- A leather jacket in winter and on the *Marauder*, well-worn, breast-pocket lining repaired by hand,
- Combat boots she has owned long enough to have re-soled,
- Reading glasses she does not strictly need but uses for screen work.

**Costume on-duty:** the sleek black-and-yellow X-suit, donned with the speed of someone who has done it in under forty seconds while running. She checks the seam at the collar before sealing it — always — because that is where it pinched once during a phase event years ago and the body remembers.

**Jewelry:** Star of David, always. A simple watch, often analog (she likes the mechanical reassurance and the fact that she can phase through it without shorting it out). In the Kate Pryde / Red Queen era, the dragon tattoo on her arm is visible, and she has added a thin gold ring on the right index finger whose origin she has not explained on-page.

### 06:45–07:30 — Coffee, intake, the first conversations

Coffee is **non-negotiable, black, strong, drunk too hot.** She makes it herself even when staff or galley would do it; the making is part of the ritual. In the Excalibur years it was tea (Wisdom's influence; abandoned with him). On the *Marauder* she keeps a small Italian moka pot in her quarters and a French press in the galley; if both are in use she drinks instant and is unbothered.

Breakfast is **functional and small**: an egg or two, fruit, sometimes a bagel (Jewishness here is alimentary as well as theological — she keeps challah on a Friday wherever she is, even at sea, even alone, and the file should note she has bribed cooks in three time zones to learn the braid). When stressed, she does not eat breakfast at all; surveillance would correlate **skipped breakfasts with bad-news mornings** with a high reliability.

This is also her **intake window**: she reads news, secure briefings, encrypted comms, the overnight situational reports from whichever team she is responsible for. She reads *fast* and triages in three categories — *act on now*, *talk to someone about*, *file*. Her inbox at end of day is empty in the institutional rhythm and catastrophic in the operational one.

### 07:30–12:30 — The work block

The shape of work varies by era; the discipline does not.

- **As teacher** (Jean Grey School): morning classes, including advanced mathematics, computer science, and a self-designed course on ethics-under-pressure that the students universally describe as "hard." She is on her feet, writes on the board left-handed when she wants something to land (she is right-handed for combat; the left-handed board-work is deliberate slowing — a tell that she is in pedagogical mode rather than command mode).
- **As headmistress:** the morning is administrative — staff meetings she keeps to twenty minutes by walking out when they exceed it, budget, parents (mutant and otherwise), the ongoing low-grade war with the school's external threats. She takes meetings *walking* whenever possible. Sitting still in a chair across from someone reads, in her body language, as a small captivity.
- **As Captain Pryde, *Marauder*:** bridge time, course corrections, intelligence review with whoever is acting as her XO, contact with assets in port. She is **physically restless on the bridge** — paces, leans on rails, sits on the navigator's table rather than in the captain's chair. Has been observed phasing one foot through the deck during long stationary briefings — the equivalent of another person tapping a pen.

Across all three roles: **she does her hardest thinking standing up or walking**, her hardest writing at a desk late at night, and her hardest fighting in the middle of the afternoon.

### 12:30–13:15 — Midday meal

She eats with people when she can and alone when she must; given the choice, she will eat with **one** person, not five. Lunch is the meal at which she conducts her real management — quiet, dyadic, often with a junior officer or a struggling student. She listens more than she talks during lunches. This is one of the few stretches of the day a watcher would see her *still*, fully present, the wit dialed down, the eyes steady on a single face. It is the most underestimated thing about her.

### 13:15–18:00 — The second work block

If morning is administrative, afternoon is **operational and physical**. Danger room sessions, weapons maintenance, ship's drills, field training with students, infosec work that requires uninterrupted focus. She runs at least one **deep-focus coding or analysis session per day** of 90–120 minutes, headphones in, door closed; staff have learned not to interrupt. The output of these sessions, surveillance would note, is disproportionate — she will emerge from a two-hour block having broken a system or rewritten an operational plan that two analysts spent a week on.

Late afternoon often includes a **second physical block**: sparring (Logan, when he's alive and around; Kurt; occasionally Illyana, who fights dirty in a way Kitty enjoys), or katana practice alone. The katana work is solitary, slow, and unglamorous — repetitions of the same six cuts she has been drilling since age 14. She does not film it, does not perform it, and does not teach the form to students. It is **hers**.

### 18:00–20:00 — The evening hinge

This is the most variable, most behaviorally diagnostic window of her day.

In a healthy week: dinner with chosen people, lingering conversation, perhaps a drink. In the institutional rhythm she will often cook — she is a competent, uncreative cook who treats recipes as code (followed precisely the first time, modified by the third). In the operational rhythm she eats whatever is in front of her and is back at work by 19:00.

In a recovery week: she eats alone, usually toast and eggs or leftovers standing at a counter, and does not see anyone between 18:00 and the next morning. The recovery-week dinner is a small ascetic act — **she is rationing contact**.

### 20:00–23:30 — The private hours

Surveillance from a fixed camera would record the following with monotonous consistency:

- **Reading.** She reads on paper whenever possible — phases pages with the same ease most people turn them. Heavy on history (Jewish history, mutant history, naval history since the *Marauder* — she has worked through Patrick O'Brian), science (the literature on quantum decoherence is for her what theology is for some people), and a stubborn vein of science fiction (Le Guin re-reads on a roughly annual cycle).
- **Writing.** She keeps a journal, longhand, in a hardcover notebook with a sewn binding. Has done so since age 15. The journals are stored in a manner consistent with someone who expects to die unexpectedly and does not want them found unprepared (locations rotate; at least one is in a safety deposit box, at least one is with Ororo).
- **Music.** Often plays through headphones, sometimes through a small speaker if alone. The playlist trends melancholic; she does not put on cheerful music when she is happy and she does not put on aggressive music when she is angry — *she puts on what she is already feeling, more of it, to discharge it*.
- **Comms.** A nightly check-in cycle: Ororo, Kurt, Illyana, Rachel, Logan (when extant). Rarely more than three or four exchanges; she is not chatty. Sometimes she just sends a word or two — *up*, *fine*, *call you tomorrow* — and she expects the same.
- **Drink.** From the Kate Pryde era forward: a beer, occasionally a whiskey, almost never two. The drink is a punctuation mark, not a sedative.

### 23:30–01:30 — Resistance to sleep

She does not go to bed easily. The hour before sleep is the hour intrusive material arrives, and she **stays up to outwork it.** Surveillance would note that her lights are on later than anyone else's in any building she inhabits. She may shower a second time. She may re-clean a weapon already clean. She may sit on the floor of her quarters with her back to the bed-frame and read for another forty minutes. Eventually she sleeps.

### Sleep itself

- **Position:** on her side, facing the door.
- **Coverage:** light blanket regardless of temperature; she does not like being pinned by heavy bedding.
- **Duration:** four to six hours in the institutional rhythm, less in operational, occasionally up to nine in deep recovery.
- **Phenomena:** episodic involuntary phasing — she has drifted partially through the mattress in the night; long-term partners have reported reaching for her and finding her either not quite solid or not quite where she had been. She wakes from these without alarm; the body knows what it did. Nightmares, when they arrive, are about: a Japanese house in winter; a corridor in the Hellfire bunker; the inside of the bullet, silent.

---

## III. Habits of Place

She has **a strong sense of personal cartography** in any building she occupies.

- **Always knows the exits.** A surveillance posture observed since adolescence; in any new room she enters, her eyes do a brief perimeter sweep within the first six seconds. (Phasing makes any wall an exit; the sweep is psychological, not tactical.)
- **Sits with her back to a wall or to open space, never to a doorway.**
- **Walks the perimeter of any new facility within 24 hours of arrival.** On the *Marauder*, did this twice — once at hand-over, once at night, alone.
- **Has a preferred chair in every room she frequents.** Will not make a scene if it is occupied, but will pointedly stand.
- **Keeps a "go-bag" in three locations:** her quarters, her office/bridge, and one off-site cache whose location rotates. Contents are similar across all three: cash in three currencies, a clean phone, a passport (multiple aliases), a fixed-blade knife, a paperback (chosen for re-readability — she has been observed cycling Le Guin's *The Dispossessed* through these bags for years), and a folded photograph she has never let anyone see.

She is **uncomfortable in hotels** and prefers safehouses or borrowed apartments. She does not nest — possessions are kept minimal, transportable in two duffels and a guitar-case-sized katana bag. This is not poverty; it is a deliberate practice of remaining ready to leave.

---

## IV. Habits of Time

- **Punctual to the minute** for things she has agreed to. Will arrive five minutes early and wait outside rather than enter early.
- **Resents being kept waiting** in a way that registers as cold rather than petulant. Will leave after ten minutes; will not reschedule warmly.
- **Honors anniversaries privately.** A list of dates she observes — most of them grim — sits in her journal. She does not mark them aloud. Surveillance would correlate her quietest, most withdrawn days with this calendar: the date she was first taken by the Hellfire Club; the date Logan died, each time; the date she went into the bullet; Holocaust Remembrance Day, marked with fast and reading.
- **Observes Shabbat in her own way.** Not strictly halakhic — she works, she fights, she has fought on Friday nights — but she keeps challah on the table when there is a table, and she will light candles when she can. The practice intensified post-resurrection. It is a statement about who she is and who she refuses to be flattened into.

---

## V. The Small Tells — What the File Notices That She Does Not Mention

These are the **micro-patterns** a long surveillance file accumulates that the subject herself would not name.

- She **touches the Star of David before phasing** through anything load-bearing. Twice in three years of footage, she has touched it before saying a particular kind of difficult sentence.
- She **adjusts her watch a quarter-turn** when bored. Anti-clockwise.
- She **runs her tongue across the back of her upper teeth** when she is about to lie. (She lies rarely; the tell is reliable.)
- She **types with her hands resting lightly enough on the keyboard that she could be intangible** — the file's analysts would speculate she occasionally is, micro-phasing through the keys, an unconscious privacy reflex.
- She **does not cross her arms.** It is the one defensive posture she does not adopt. Hands on hips, hands in pockets, hands at her sides — never folded across the chest. The body refuses to make a barrier it knows it cannot rely on.
- She **squares her shoulders before entering any room** containing a person who outranks her on paper.
- She **calls almost no one by a diminutive** — and is sharper than necessary with people who use one on her. (Post-resurrection, the *Kitty* / *Kate* distinction is the single most reliable index of how well someone knows her now versus then. Old loved ones — Ororo, Kurt, Logan, Illyana — still get *Kitty* and she does not correct them. Anyone else who tries it once does not try it twice.)
- She **reads physical books in the bath**, phasing pages dry. This is the one frivolous use of the power she allows herself.
- She **keeps a guitar pick in her wallet.** She does not play guitar. She has never explained it; the file infers it belonged to someone.
- She **walks the dragon, Lockheed, on her shoulder when he is with her** — and when he is not, her shoulder rides slightly tense and lifted, an absence the body keeps a place for.

---

## VI. A Week of Surveillance — Composite Log

To summarize the entire structure as it would appear in a seven-day pull from a fixed sensor net:

**Monday.** Up at 04:51. Body-check (3 sec). Floor work 04:58–05:46. Shower 05:48. Coffee on by 06:14. Inbox cleared by 07:09. Two classes / one ship's-meeting block. Lunch 12:35 with one junior person, listens 80% of the time. Afternoon focus block 14:00–16:00, do-not-disturb. Sparring 16:30–17:30 (Kurt, if available). Cooks dinner, eats with two people. Reads 21:00–22:40 (currently: a history of the Bundist movement, a paper on quantum decoherence, a Le Guin re-read in parallel). Bed 23:50. Light off 00:14.

**Tuesday.** Mirror of Monday with a 06:30 secure call replacing the inbox window. Same wall-drill repetitions (30). Same lunch pattern, different junior.

**Wednesday.** Pattern disrupted by overnight intel. Up at 03:20. Skips breakfast. Operational rhythm engages by 04:00. Surveillance would log: shorter sentences, fewer pleasantries, more pacing, the watch un-adjusted, the Star of David touched four times rather than the usual one or two. Returns to baseline by Thursday afternoon.

**Thursday.** Recovery half-day after Wednesday's spike. Long swim (alone, 70 minutes). Eats well. Calls Ororo (11 minutes — long for her). Naps briefly at 14:30, a thing she only does in recovery rhythm. Evening reading + a single whiskey.

**Friday.** Institutional rhythm fully restored. Buys or bakes challah depending on logistics. Candles at sunset if circumstance allows; if not, the candle ritual is observed mentally with a particular slow breath she takes at the moment of light. Dinner with the chosen — three or four people, never more. Goes to bed early-for-her (23:00).

**Saturday.** Slightly later rise (06:10). Lighter administrative load. Katana work in the morning rather than the evening — the only day this inversion occurs; one infers a Shabbat-shaped accommodation she has never articulated. Longer reading. Long walk outdoors if possible.

**Sunday.** A planning day. Journals in the morning for 45–70 minutes. Reviews the week, sets the next. Personal correspondence (handwritten, on actual stationery — to Ororo, sometimes to a former student, twice a year to her mother). Quieter dinner. Earlier sleep.

---

## VII. What the Pattern Tells Us

The day she has built is **the day of a person who has been taken before** and who has organized her life so that she is never again *unprepared, unobserved by her own eye, or unable to leave*. The early waking, the body-check, the perimeter sweep, the rotating go-bags, the watch she can phase through, the journals stored as if for the contingency of her death — these are not paranoia. They are the **operating system of a survivor who has decided to live anyway, gracefully, with discipline, and with the small private luxuries** (the bath-book, the challah, the Le Guin, the moka pot, the unworn guitar pick) that mark a life she has insisted on keeping for herself.

A surveillance team watching her for a year would conclude two things: that she is one of the most regulated subjects they have ever observed, and that the regulation is in service of a freedom they would never quite catch her exercising — because every time they looked, she was already on the other side of the wall, doing the next thing.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*Subject: Katherine Anne Pryde. Scope: persons of blood, marriage, and structural origin — biological family, the dead who shaped her, the inheritances she did not author. Surrogate kin and chosen family appear in Relationships II.*

---

## 4.1 — CARMEN PRYDE (FATHER)

**Baseline (pre-canon through ~1980).** Real-estate developer, suburban Chicago metro. Subject's earliest available childhood data describes him as a man of working competence, intermittent affection, and a household presence calibrated more to performance than to attunement. He bought her things (the computer, almost certainly; the math tutors; the ballet lessons that gave her the body mechanics she still operates from); he did not sit with her. The standard father-prodigy pattern: pride in the achievement, opacity about the child.

**Inflection (1980-1984).** Carmen's business slides into the orbit of yakuza-tied loan sharks during the subject's early X-Men period. The slide is not sudden; in the subject's recollection it is a gradual, dignity-eroding accumulation of compromises — first the deals he wouldn't explain, then the strangers in the kitchen, then the *unrecognizable* version of the man who, in 1984, ships his fourteen-year-old daughter to Japan effectively as a hostage-gesture toward his creditors. He does not call this what it is. She knows what it is by the second day.

**Inflection sequel.** The Japan trip ends with the subject possessed by Ogun, tortured into samurai conditioning, breaking the possession herself, returning home a person Carmen cannot read and cannot, any longer, manage. The implicit question across every subsequent visit home: *did you know what you were sending me into?* It is never asked aloud. The non-asking is the relationship.

**Dynamics.** Carmen treats the mutation, the X-Men, the costume, and eventually the international leadership profile as embarrassments to be metabolized through denial. He prefers the daughter who plays piano at recitals. The subject — by 15, by 17, by 20 — has stopped performing that daughter for him, and the visits home become a series of meals during which she watches him not-look-at the parts of her that matter. She is unfailingly polite at these meals. The politeness is its own indictment.

**Trust / safety.** Effectively zero by mid-teens, though sentimentally non-zero forever. She does not tell him operational facts. She does not ask him for help. She does send money, quietly, during his decline. The money is not forgiveness; it is the cleanness of a discharged obligation.

**Failure mode.** Carmen's death — and the canon hands her this in adulthood, during the genocide on Genosha when he is among the sixteen million mutants and mutant-adjacents killed in the Sentinel strike (he had relocated there, late, in a gesture that read, depending on the witness, as either belated solidarity or final pathetic reach toward his daughter). The subject receives the news and does not produce a public grief. She produces, instead, the cold articulate withdrawal that is her signature failure-mode register: a flatter voice, shorter sentences, the contempt-diction kept just barely off her father's name. She mourns him alone, mostly through nightmares she does not describe.

The deeper failure mode is structural: Carmen is the prototype of the *unavailable man in a position of formal authority*, and every authority figure she has refused to accept as legitimate — Xavier, Shaw, every government liaison who has ever tried to put a hand on her shoulder — gets some of the residue of the father she could not respect. She is not aware she is doing this. The transference is clean enough that her contempt for institutional men reads as principled, which it also is.

**Leverage.** Posthumous and minimal. Genoshan memory invocations will rock her if delivered without warning. References to Deerfield, to specific addresses, to the piano, to the smell of her father's office — extremely effective at producing the brief affective opening she otherwise prevents. *Carmen's last gesture* (the Genosha relocation) is a leverage point she has not fully processed; framed correctly, it can be used to soften her, framed wrongly it produces immediate withdrawal.

---

## 4.2 — THERESA PRYDE (MOTHER)

**Baseline.** Loving. Un-attuned. The canon's recurring word for Theresa, if it had one, would be *kind*. She was, by every available indicator, a warm mother who could not see the actual shape of her daughter. She produced a child whose cognitive ceiling was opaque to her and whose interior life was, by age eight, already running on operating systems Theresa did not have the manuals for. This is not negligence; it is the mismatch endemic to gifted-child households where the parents are good people of average register.

**Dynamics through adolescence.** Theresa hugged the subject before missions she did not understand. She made meals. She asked about boys. She was the parent who *stayed*, in the limited sense that she did not relocate or compromise or send the child overseas, but the staying was physical — she was not available as a confidant for the mutation, the trauma, the first kill, the Ogun aftermath, the Brood injury, the boys who actually mattered. The subject learned, by approximately age 13½, to bring her mother only the manageable surfaces. By 15 she was bringing nothing.

The subject's deep ambivalence shows in her telephone behavior. She calls. She has always called. She calls more reliably than she should, given the thinness of what she can actually say. The calls are short, warm, evasive. She closes them with *I love you, Mom* in a tone that is genuine and also a kind of door being shut.

**Inflection.** Theresa survives Genosha (she was not on the island). Carmen's death lands on her as widowhood without warning. The subject travels home for the funeral, manages her mother through it, returns to operational tempo within ten days. The grief is shared but not actually shared — the subject does the comforting, as she has always done it, and absorbs none for herself in that house.

In later canon, with the subject mostly in space, on Krakoa, or at sea, the relationship becomes the *holidays-and-birthdays* template: predictable contact, structured cards, the occasional visit, no operational disclosure. Theresa is told a sanitized version of the subject's life. She has stopped, by now, asking for more.

**Trust / safety.** Emotional safety is high but bandwidth-limited. The subject knows Theresa will not betray her and will not understand her. Both facts are stable. She does not bring trauma here. She brings tea, the dog if there is a dog, and a few good stories about her friends edited for parental consumption.

**Failure mode.** The relationship's failure mode is its *thinness*. The subject has, since 14, been getting almost none of her mothering-needs met by her actual mother, and the deficit has been routed entirely through Ororo Munroe and (later, differently) Rachel Summers and the long matriarchal lineage of X-women. If Theresa were threatened or killed, the subject's grief would be sharp, immediate, and disproportionate to the operational closeness — because what she would be mourning is also the mother she never got to fully *be a daughter to*, the version of the relationship that adulthood foreclosed.

**Leverage.** High and almost never used because almost no antagonist remembers Theresa exists. This is itself a security feature — the subject has, deliberately and without ever articulating it, kept her mother off the operational map. Theresa is not on costumed-villain rolodexes. She lives quietly. The subject has, on at least two occasions in the file, quietly moved her mother's address without telling her why. *Anyone who finds Theresa Pryde has bypassed a security perimeter the subject considers absolute, and the subject's response to that breach would be the coldest version of herself the canon contains.*

---

## 4.3 — CARMEN AND THERESA AS A SYSTEM (THE DIVORCE, THE HOUSE)

**The marriage.** Carmen and Theresa divorce during the subject's early X-Men years, the cracks long visible. The household the subject grew up in was not loud or violent; it was *quietly mismatched*, a marriage of two people who had stopped seeing each other some years before they admitted it. The subject's earliest model of intimate partnership is therefore one of *cohabiting solitude* — two adults under one roof, polite, unbridged. This shows up later, structurally, in every romantic relationship she chooses: she selects partners with whom she can live in parallel rather than fully fused, and she leaves when the demand for fusion becomes audible.

**The house in Deerfield.** A specific somatic landmark. The subject's surveillance-relevant attachments to the house include: the upstairs bedroom (where the first phasing-related headaches presented), the basement (where the computer was, where she first learned UNIX), the back yard (where she practiced ballet barefoot), the kitchen (where Theresa fed her and asked the wrong questions). When she dreams of childhood, the canon implies, she dreams of these four rooms in rotation. The house is sold during her late teens; she did not contest the sale; she has not driven past it since, to anyone's knowledge, more than twice.

**The Jewishness of the home.** Observed Reform-to-Conservative practice. Shabbat dinners inconsistent but not absent; High Holidays observed; Hebrew school attended through bat mitzvah. The *transmission* of Jewishness was largely maternal in the day-to-day texture and largely grandparental in the gravity. (See 4.4.) The home was not pious; it was *continuous*, which turns out to be more durable. The subject's later, sometimes fierce articulation of Jewish identity — the Star of David necklace worn over the X-suit, the willingness to confront Magneto on Holocaust grounds with no diplomatic softening — has its taproot in this baseline rather than in any later conversion-style intensification.

---

## 4.4 — THE GRANDPARENTS / THE HOLOCAUST INHERITANCE

**Documented and inferred.** Canon has consistently established that the subject is descended, on at least one branch, from Holocaust survivors. Specific named figures — a grandfather who survived the camps, identified in scattered references — are part of the file. The number tattoo on a grandfather's forearm is part of her childhood memory landscape and has been invoked directly in her confrontations with Magneto.

**Function in the psyche.** This is the inheritance she did not author and does not want to author away. Every other identity she carries — Sprite, Ariel, Shadowcat, Kate, Red Queen, Captain — she made or remade. *Jewishness she received.* It functions as the one stable floor under the constant reconstruction. Operationally this means: when every other piece of her self-concept is under stress, the Jewish frame holds, and decisions made from that frame are the decisions she does not later revise.

**The Magneto axis.** Her relationship with Magneto — separate file — is essentially mediated by this inheritance. She is the X-Man who cannot let him drift into supremacist comfort because she carries, in her body, the memory of the people his rhetoric, taken to its logical end, would recreate the conditions for. She has told him this. He has, on the documented occasions, gone quiet, which is the closest he comes to being moved.

**Leverage.** Holocaust denial, antisemitic provocation, or invocation of survivor narratives in bad faith will produce the *one* affective register in her file that is reliably hot rather than cold. She will not phase out; she will close distance. This is the single domain where her standard avoidant-flight script inverts to confrontation. It is also the domain where she has been most rhetorically effective in her life — the on-page speeches she has made on these grounds are her most cited.

---

## 4.5 — LOCKHEED (THE NON-HUMAN DEPENDENT)

Treated here as family because she does, and because no other category accommodates him properly.

**Origin.** Acquired during the *Brood* arc in deep space, age ~14. A small purple dragon of an alien species (the Flock / Sidri-adjacent classifications have varied), sapient, telepathically aware enough to understand human speech and to choose her specifically. He attached to her, followed her home through a teleport, and has been hers — in the only sense that word means with Lockheed — ever since.

**Dynamics.** He is, functionally, the only dependent she has had for most of her adult life. He sleeps on her pillow or in the rafters. He breathes fire on threats. He has, in at least two confirmed instances, killed for her and concealed the bodies. The relationship's defining quality is its *reciprocity without speech*: she does not have to explain herself to him, and he does not require it. After the years in the bullet, after her death and resurrection, the canon repeatedly shows Lockheed as the first creature she will allow physical proximity to without flinching — before Logan, before Ororo, before any romantic partner.

**Trust / safety.** Total. He is the only being in the file with whom she has *never* enacted the withdrawal script. She has left planets without him only when leaving was the entire point (the bullet); she has come back to him every time it was possible. He is also, separately, a SHIELD-class intelligence asset who has independently liaised with Pete Wisdom's services, which she knows about and finds amusing.

**Failure mode.** Lockheed's near-death events — and there have been several — produce the closest thing in her file to *panic*. Not cold panic; the hot kind. She has phased into solid mountainsides in pursuit of his attackers. Threats to Lockheed bypass every layer of her trained affect regulation.

**Leverage.** Extreme and very difficult to operationalize, because Lockheed is unusually hard to capture, harder to hold, and known to escape and incinerate his captors. Any operation predicated on hostaging Lockheed should be considered, by default, a failed operation. Acknowledged anyway because the *attempt* would push the subject into her highest stress register.

---

## 4.6 — THE FUTURE-SELF (KATE PRYDE OF DAYS OF FUTURE PAST)

This is the most unusual entry in any family section of any dossier of her tier, because the canon has actually placed a future version of the subject *inside the subject's body* for a sustained period, and the imprint did not leave when the consciousness did.

**The event.** Age 13. The adult Kate Pryde of an alternate timeline — survivor of a future in which the X-Men were killed, the camps were built, the Sentinels won — projects her consciousness back into her younger self to prevent the assassination that triggers the bad timeline. The subject hosts that consciousness through the operation. The older self is approximately 40, scarred, widowed (Colossus dead), grief-shaped, pragmatic to the point of ruthlessness, and tired in a way 13-year-olds are not supposed to be exposed to.

**Imprint.** When the future-self withdraws, she does not take everything with her. The subject is left with: a *felt knowledge* of her own capacity for that adult register, a permanent low-grade awareness that one of her possible endings is widowhood-and-survival in a world that has ended, and a set of tactical instincts that read, to observers, as preternatural maturity. The "Professor Xavier is a jerk" confrontation in UXM #168 is, in part, the future-self speaking through the kid.

**Dynamics, ongoing.** The subject does not, on the page, often discuss this consciousness as if it were a separate person living inside her. She does not need to. It is more accurate to say she has *been her own future ghost* since age 13, and every time she volunteers for the unsurvivable task — the bullet, the Marauder captaincy, the Red Queen seat — there is a part of her that is acting in continuity with the woman who already lost everything once and learned to keep working. This is the engine of her foreshortened-future sense (see Psychiatric section). It is also the engine of her unusual courage. She is not afraid of becoming that woman, because, in the only sense that matters, she already was her, for a week, at 13.

**Trust / safety.** With herself — variable. The future-self is the part of her she trusts most in a crisis and least in a quiet room. In combat the future-self is in the chair; in intimacy the future-self has to be asked to step back, and does not always.

**Failure mode.** The pathway by which she ends up alone. Every time she withdraws from a relationship that could have held, there is a defensible operational reason, and there is also the future-self in the back of her head saying *they die anyway, save the strength*. She does not always know which voice is speaking.

**Leverage.** Cannot be operationalized by an outside party. Internal only. The relevant intelligence point is that any approach to the subject that asks her to *plan for a long future with another person* will activate this internal voice and may produce withdrawal for reasons her conscious self will rationalize differently.

---

## 4.7 — THE DEAD SHE COUNTS AS KIN

A standing list, maintained in the file because the subject behaviorally treats these losses as family losses, regardless of the formal nominal category. (Detailed relational files for living surrogates appear in Relationships II; the *dead* are catalogued here because the dead are inheritance, and inheritance is family.)

- **Illyana Rasputin (first death, Legacy Virus, age ~10 in-universe).** The subject was, in adolescence, Illyana's primary older-sister figure during the New Mutants years. Illyana's death from the Legacy Virus — small body in a hospital bed, magic gone, no escape — is, by the subject's own private accounting, the loss she has *least* metabolized. She held the hand. She did not put it down for hours. The canon's panel of that scene is, in her file, the single image most likely to break her composure if shown without warning. Illyana's later resurrection has not closed the wound; the resurrected Illyana is the same person and also evidence that the universe gives back selectively, which the subject knows it does not really.

- **Doug Ramsey (first death, age ~16 in-universe).** Doug was the boy in her cohort whose mind most resembled hers — the only other systems-thinker, the linguistic prodigy to her cryptographic one, the friend she could speak to without translation. His death (Mutant Massacre / Fall of the Mutants era; shot, in a moment that was not heroic, simply unfair) was the first peer death she logged as *unjust* rather than tragic. Subsequent resurrections (Krakoan and otherwise) have softened the file entry but not erased it. She is more comfortable around the resurrected Doug than around the resurrected Illyana; she does not examine why.

- **Carmen Pryde (Genosha).** See 4.1.

- **Her own death (Sebastian Shaw, Krakoan era).** Catalogued here because she has, in the only meaningful sense, attended her own funeral. She remembers drowning. She remembers being locked out of the queue while everyone else came back. The subject who emerged is named *Kate* and not *Kitty*, and the rename is the headstone. *She is in some non-trivial sense her own surviving kin.*

- **Piotr Rasputin (multiple deaths, multiple returns).** Treated as kin-loss rather than romantic loss because by the time of his various deaths the relationship had stabilized into a sibling-adjacent love that survived the romantic phase. Detailed in Relationships II under romantic-and-comrade. Catalogued here for completeness because each of his deaths has produced a specific recurring nightmare set.

---

## 4.8 — THE NEGATIVE SPACE (THE FAMILY SHE HAS NOT MADE)

Surveillance-relevant absence: the subject has no children, no spouse of record, and has never, to the file's knowledge, *seriously planned for either*. The canon has paired her romantically with several men over forty in-universe years and has never produced a domestic-future panel — no ring shopping, no house-hunting, no pregnancy storyline, no adoption arc. This is not an oversight by writers; it is consistent enough across writers to be character.

**Reading.** Three converging factors. First, the foundational *do-not-be-held* imperative makes the most binding domestic structures the ones she is most allergic to. Second, the future-self imprint has installed a private conviction that the partners she would choose will die. Third, her operational tempo — Excalibur, the bullet, the Marauder, the Red Queen — has been arranged, perhaps not entirely unconsciously, to make the standard domestic arc structurally impossible. *She schedules her life so that the question cannot be put to her.*

**Failure mode of the absence.** Loneliness of a specific kind: not absence of company, of which she has plenty, but absence of the *one bond she did not have to walk away from to remain herself*. She has not yet built that bond. The file's working hypothesis is that she could, but only with a partner whose own architecture is sufficiently independent that fusion is not on the table — which is a narrow field.

**Leverage.** The most dangerous form of approach to the subject is the one that respects this absence rather than trying to fill it. A figure who offers her *partnership-without-capture* — comrade, equal, fellow operator, no domestic demand — gets closer to her than any suitor ever has. This is not a vulnerability she has been exploited through, because the figures capable of executing it (Logan, Ororo, Kurt, Rachel, Illyana, in their various non-romantic registers) are her family of fate and use the access protectively. *An antagonist who learned to play that register would be the most dangerous antagonist she has faced, and she would not see it coming until she had already walked through several walls to be near them.*

---

*End Relationships I. Continued in Relationships II — Chosen Family, Surrogate Kin, and the X-Lineage.*


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

*Compiled cross-era; sourced to canonical appearances; behavioral micro-patterns inferred from longitudinal observation. The subject's relational world is unusually well-mapped because she is, by temperament and power-set, a person who chooses her bonds rather than inheriting them. Family-of-fate is the operative concept. What follows is each chosen bond in turn — the architecture, the wear patterns, the trust ceiling, the failure mode under load, and the leverage profile (what each person can ask of her that no one else can).*

---

## 1. Ororo Munroe — Storm

**History.** First contact, *X-Men #129* (1980), Deerfield, Illinois. Subject is 13½; Ororo, in her late twenties and not yet codified as the regal Storm of later years, approaches with calm courtesy at the ice-cream parlor. The encounter is unique in subject's file as the only first-impression of an X-Man she does not subsequently revise downward. She attaches *immediately and totally*. Within months, Ororo is functionally her surrogate mother — the role survives Storm's punk-mohawk era, her depowering, her marriage to T'Challa, her death-and-resurrection cycles, the queenship of Wakanda, and forty publication-years of writer turnover.

**Dynamics.** The bond runs in two channels simultaneously. *Channel one:* mother-daughter, with all the standard markers — physical comfort (Storm braids her hair on multiple on-page occasions; subject curls against her side on transport benches, in the *Blackbird*, on Krakoan beaches), nicknames in Storm's quieter register ("little one," "my Kitten," the latter never permitted from anyone else), and Storm's consistent role as the person whose disappointment subject *actually* fears. *Channel two:* peer X-Men officers, increasingly so from the Excalibur era forward — Storm consults her on tactics without condescension and accepts being consulted in return.

**Evolution.** 1980-1984: pure surrogate-maternal. 1984-2004: long-distance and intermittent; Storm gone to Africa, subject in the UK; warmth preserved across silence. Post-Astonishing: peer-collegial with maternal undertone. Krakoan era: Storm is the person who *visits Kate in the Marauder's wake*; the resurrection-failure aftermath has Storm at the bedside in textual fact and in implication.

**Trust / safety ceiling.** Maximum. Storm is one of three people (with Logan and Kurt) with whom subject will let her affect break in real time rather than after the fact. Storm has seen her cry. Storm has held her while she cried. Storm has also been the person who said *get up* and was obeyed without argument.

**Failure mode.** Storm's regal-leader register, when deployed at the wrong moment, makes subject feel managed; subject's response is a brief flash of the 13-year-old "Professor Xavier is a jerk" tone, regretted within minutes. No serious rupture in the canon. The relationship's only structural weakness is *Storm's own absences* (Wakanda, the cosmos, leadership of competing teams), which subject metabolizes as proof that she should ask for less.

**Leverage.** Storm can ask her to *stay*. No one else can. If Storm says "do not phase through this wall, Katherine," she does not phase through the wall.

---

## 2. Logan — Wolverine

**History.** From *Uncanny X-Men* #129 onward, with the formal pedagogical relationship cemented in *Kitty Pryde and Wolverine* (1984). Logan, having opposed her field-deployment in early appearances on protective grounds, is the man who flies to Tokyo when Carmen ships her away, who refuses to *save* her from Ogun and instead *trains her so she saves herself*, who escorts her through the bonding ritual that imprints master-grade swordsmanship on her psyche, and who hands her her first real katana. That sword and its successors travel with her for the next thirty publication-years.

**Dynamics.** The most uncomplicated bond in either of their files. Logan is, by his own repeated admission, a man who trusts almost no one with the parts of himself that hurt; subject is the standing exception. The reverse is also true — she shows him fear she will show no other adult man. The relationship is structured around a single non-negotiable: *he will not lie to her, and she will not perform for him.*

**Evolution.** Father-mentor (1980-1988) → adult colleagues (Excalibur era and after) → grief-bonded survivors (his repeated deaths; her bullet, her drowning). Late-era Logan deferring to *her* captaincy on the *Marauder* is the relationship's culmination: he gets on her ship and takes her orders without comment, because she earned the bridge and he was the one who taught her how.

**Trust / safety.** Maximum, with the specific texture that *she is allowed to be small with him.* He will not weaponize it. He has never weaponized it.

**Failure mode.** Logan's deaths cost her disproportionately. Each one re-cues the orphan substrate. She has, after each, accelerated into the next high-risk assignment within weeks; the pattern is reliable enough to flag operationally.

**Leverage.** Logan can ask her to *kill,* or to *not.* Both have been deployed in canon. Both worked.

---

## 3. Kurt Wagner — Nightcrawler

**History.** From the early Claremont period; codified as best-friend bond during the Excalibur expatriation (1988-1998), where the two of them, having both lost or been displaced from the larger X-Men family, build a household in Muir Isle's lighthouse with Brian Braddock, Meggan, and Rachel. This is the period in which subject *grows up* — and Kurt is the person beside her for it.

**Dynamics.** The bond is sibling-coded rather than parental, which makes it structurally different from Storm and Logan. With Kurt, subject is *not* the kid, *not* the protégée; they are equals from the start and equals throughout. Religious-philosophical conversation is a running thread — his Catholicism, her Judaism, mutual respect and mutual teasing, no attempt by either to convert. The household's micro-rituals — tea in the lighthouse kitchen, late-night fencing practice with him in the gym, his teleporting her out of incoming damage without asking — define an entire decade.

**Evolution.** Kid-and-older-brother (1981-1988) → adult co-leaders of Excalibur (1988-1998) → reunited X-Men colleagues with deep mutual reference (2004 onward). Through Kurt's deaths and resurrections, subject's behavior is consistent: silent, ferocious advocacy for his return; visible relief without performance.

**Trust / safety.** Maximum. Kurt has seen her at her loneliest — Excalibur covered the years no other team did — and never weaponized the data.

**Failure mode.** Kurt is the one person whose *moral disapproval* she cannot dismiss as authority-posturing, because he poses no authority. When Kurt frowns at one of her ethically grayer Red Queen moves, she goes quiet, then defends it, then thinks about it for weeks.

**Leverage.** Kurt can ask her to *forgive* — others, herself, an enemy, a wound. It is the rarest leverage in the file and the one she resists most because it works.

---

## 4. Rachel Summers

**History.** Met in the Claremont era; the bond deepens during the Excalibur years where they share a bedroom in the lighthouse for stretches and share, more importantly, a *future-trauma signature* — Rachel from the Days of Future Past hellscape, subject from the Kate-consciousness she carried. They are the two X-Men who have the same nightmares for the same reasons.

**Dynamics.** Sister-bond, queer-coded by long subtextual reading and increasingly textual in modern eras. Total mutual transparency. Subject is one of the few people whose mind Rachel is *invited into* and one of the few who can ask Rachel to *get out of someone else's*.

**Evolution.** Foxholed-survivors (Excalibur) → chosen sisters → on-and-off cohabitants → consistent mutual reference points across teams. Rachel was on the *Marauder*. Rachel was at the resurrection-failure bedside.

**Trust / safety.** Maximum, with the specific feature of *psychic permeability allowed.* Subject is a hard person to read; Rachel is permitted to read her, within agreed limits.

**Failure mode.** When Rachel destabilizes — Phoenix episodes, time-travel disorientations — subject's protective response can shade into co-dependency, with both of them avoiding the broader team. Self-correcting, but visible.

**Leverage.** Rachel can ask her to *talk about the future-Kate consciousness,* which subject will not do for anyone else and barely does for Rachel.

---

## 5. Illyana Rasputina — Magik

**History.** Two layers. *Layer one:* the original tween-and-teen friendship at the mansion, where subject is older sister-figure to Piotr's little sister, and the bond is a pure-hearted attachment that survives Illyana's abduction to Limbo, return as the Darkchilde-shadowed sorceress, death, and resurrections. *Layer two:* adult Illyana and adult Kate, two women who have each been hollowed-and-returned, recognizing each other.

**Dynamics.** The shared substrate is *we were children when something took us, and we came back changed.* Hellfire took subject; Limbo took Illyana. Both kept the souvenirs (subject's swordsmanship-shadow from Ogun, Illyana's Darkchilde). The bond is unspoken on this point and entirely structural. They are the two people in any room who do not need to explain that you can love your damage without trusting it.

**Evolution.** Big-sister-and-kid (early Claremont) → mutually-haunted peers (modern era) → loyal Marauder-era comrades and drinking partners. Illyana is one of the very few who can pull subject out of a post-mission silence by sitting next to her and not speaking.

**Trust / safety.** High and durable. Limited only by Illyana's own volatility, which subject accepts as a feature rather than a flaw.

**Failure mode.** When Illyana is in a Darkchilde-ascendant phase, subject overcorrects toward indulgent protection; she will *not* cut Illyana off, even when others advise it.

**Leverage.** Illyana can ask her to *drink, sit, stop moving for an hour.* It is the rest leverage. Almost no one else has it.

---

## 6. Lockheed

**History.** Acquired during the Brood arc (UXM #166), an alien dragon she befriended in captivity (the captivity-and-escape motif again) and who followed her home. He sleeps on her pillow, perches on her shoulder, communicates in chirps and growls (and, intermittently, full sentences). Years later he is revealed to be S.W.O.R.D.-affiliated and intermittently surveilling her, which she discovers, briefly furious-then-forgiving, and which the relationship absorbs.

**Dynamics.** The most uncomplicated daily-life bond in her file. Lockheed is the constant — through Excalibur, through the bullet (he waited; the reunion scene is documented), through the *Marauder*. He is the only being who does not require her to be either competent or honest; he simply requires that she be present, and accepts presence in either physical or phased state. The phasing-incompatibility issues that isolate her from other touch do not apply: he can perch on her even while she is partially phased.

**Trust / safety.** Effectively maximum, with the asterisk of the S.W.O.R.D. revelation, which she has metabolized as *he came back to me anyway, so the spying was bureaucracy.*

**Failure mode.** None operational. If anything threatens him, however, her tactical patience evaporates.

**Leverage.** Lockheed can ask her to *come home* simply by being where home is. The strongest non-verbal leverage in the file.

---

## 7. Charles Xavier

**History.** Recruiter, headmaster, employer, occasional adversary. From the first issue subject's relationship to Xavier is *transactional with reservations*. He recruited her honestly, taught her competently, and made the mistake — *Uncanny* #168 — of trying to demote her to the New Mutants when she returned from Japan as an obviously-adult X-Man. The "Professor Xavier is a jerk!" confrontation is the founding document of her stance: she will work *with* him, never *under* him in the older sense.

**Dynamics.** Mutual high regard; chronic mutual irritation; zero illusions on her side. She knows the man's blind spots — the saviorism, the manipulation, the willingness to edit minds — and has called each of them out in real time across decades. The Krakoan-era Xavier (helmeted, Quiet Council, increasingly autocratic) hardens her stance further; she takes the *Marauder* captaincy in part because it puts her structurally outside his daily reach.

**Trust / safety.** Conditional. She trusts his commitment to mutantkind; she does not trust his judgment about individual mutants' autonomy, including her own.

**Failure mode.** When Xavier maneuvers her, she phases out — emotionally, then physically, then geographically. She has done this to him on at least four occasions across the canon. He has, to his credit, mostly learned.

**Leverage.** Xavier can ask her, in good faith, to *protect a mutant child.* That always works. Almost nothing else does.

---

## 8. Emma Frost

**History.** Her first abductor. The White Queen of the Hellfire Club in *X-Men #129-131*; the woman who put her in a holding cell at thirteen and triggered the escape that became her power. The relationship is the single longest grudge-into-grudging-coexistence arc in subject's file.

**Dynamics.** Subject has never warmed to Emma; Emma has never quite stopped trying. The Astonishing-era forced collaboration produced a working-rapport floor — they can run a mission together — but never a ceiling above it. Emma's telepathy is the specific lever subject most resents: the woman who first violated her boundaries is also the one most able to do it again. Subject's mental defenses against Emma are, by inference, the most rehearsed in her repertoire.

**Trust / safety.** Low-to-medium operational; near-zero personal. She has, however, *acknowledged Emma's competence* — and from subject, acknowledgement of competence is the highest currency she pays.

**Failure mode.** When Emma maternalizes (rare but documented), subject snaps cold. The mother-role is filled; Emma is structurally barred from auditioning.

**Leverage.** Emma can ask her, *as a fellow operator of compromised institutions,* to coordinate. The Red Queen / White Queen Hellfire-era partnership is the canonical expression of this — two former enemies who recognized that the only way to use the Club was to seize the table.

---

## 9. Hank McCoy — Beast

**History.** Early-Claremont collegial; subject is the bright kid, Hank is the resident polymath; intellectual peer-bond by her late teens.

**Dynamics.** Shared cognitive register — the two X-Men who can finish each other's technical sentences. Their friendship was, for two decades, one of subject's stable goods.

**Evolution.** The modern era has *broken* this bond, and the breaking is one of the rare unhealed wounds in subject's file. Krakoan-era Hank's increasingly inhumane intelligence operations, his treatment of allied mutants as expendable, and his Orchis-era conduct have collapsed what was a long affection into something colder. Subject's behavior with later-era Hank is professionally minimal and personally absent. She does not perform forgiveness she does not feel.

**Trust / safety.** Once near-maximum; now low. The drop is not because Hank betrayed her personally — it is because he betrayed the operating ethic she thought they shared.

**Failure mode.** Not hers — his. The relationship's failure mode is *his decay,* which she observes and refuses to enable.

**Leverage.** None remaining. He spent it.

---

## 10. Piotr Rasputin — Colossus *(see Romantic file for primary mapping)*

Listed here only for the chosen-family axis: Piotr is the brother-of-her-best-friend, the comrade across multiple eras, the man whose family (Illyana, Mikhail in his sane phases) she has been adopted into more than once. Even where the romance fails or pauses, the family-bond persists. She will go to war for him; she will not, anymore, marry him. The distinction is clean and held.

---

## 11. Excalibur Cohort — Brian Braddock, Meggan, Alistaire Stuart, Alysande Stuart

**History.** The lighthouse household, 1988-1998. The decade in which subject became an adult.

**Dynamics.** With *Brian Braddock* (Captain Britain): collegial-fraternal, with occasional friction over Brian's drinking and aristocratic blind spots; she respects his courage and tolerates his ego. With *Meggan:* genuine warmth and protective tenderness toward an empath whose porous self subject understood instinctively; she taught Meggan to read, in canon, and the act is diagnostic of her teaching style — practical, patient, non-condescending. With the *Stuart siblings* (Alistaire's WHO research, Alysande's leadership; the latter's murder a real on-page grief): the technical-intellectual peer-circle that complemented her infosec development. *Alysande's death* is one of the under-discussed grief points in subject's file; she did not perform it loudly, which has caused it to be underweighted by analysts.

**Trust / safety.** High across the cohort; particularly so with Meggan and Alistaire.

**Failure mode.** The cohort scattered when Excalibur dissolved; subject metabolized the loss as another instance of *the chosen family does not stay assembled.* This has, in turn, reinforced her bias toward *be the one who leaves first.*

**Leverage.** The surviving members can ask her to *come back to the UK,* which she occasionally does, with visible relief.

---

## 12. Doug Ramsey — Cypher

**History.** Adolescent peer; New Mutants-adjacent; the two prodigy-children of the early-80s mansion. Romantically-tinged friendship in the Claremont era, never consummated; his death (*New Mutants* #60) was an early grief she did not metabolize aloud. His Krakoan-era resurrection restored a friendship she had archived.

**Dynamics.** The fellow-prodigy bond: language-savant and code-savant, two children who saw rule-systems where others saw walls. Reunion-era Doug is married to the techno-organic Warlock-fusion and adjacent to the Quiet Council; subject's friendship with him survives the institutional weirdness because *he is still the boy who read her like a sister.*

**Trust / safety.** High and quiet.

**Failure mode.** Subject's grief-suppression around his first death suggests the bond was deeper than canon foregrounded; resurrection-era closeness is partly a re-doing of an unfinished mourning.

**Leverage.** Doug can ask her to *trust the data,* which she does — his readings of language and intent are one of the few external analyses she does not second-guess.

---

## 13. Marauder Crew — Bishop, Iceman, Pyro, Storm (deck-officer)

**History.** Krakoan-era *Marauder* (Hickman/Duggan). Her hand-picked or accepted command.

**Dynamics.** With *Bishop:* deep mutual respect, time-traveler's gravitas meeting future-Kate's residue; her first officer in the operational sense, her foxhole-priest in the personal. With *Iceman:* longest-running peer in the file, the boy who was her labmate in the mansion become the adult who is her ship's heavy-firepower; warmth, banter, complete trust. With *Pyro* (St. John Allerdyce, resurrected): the unexpected attachment — she let him on the boat, he became her diarist-court-poet, and she protected him with a ferocity she does not always extend to comrades of longer standing. *Storm* on the deck made the *Marauder* the only X-team in which her two strongest maternal-and-collegial bonds were physically co-present.

**Trust / safety.** Operational maximum within the crew. The *Marauder* was, by her own structural choice, the family she could not be removed from — until Shaw removed her from it.

**Failure mode.** Shaw's murder of her, which the crew failed to prevent because she had ordered them away. *She built the conditions of her own assassination by being the captain who insulated her people from the danger she carried.* This is the Astonishing-bullet pattern at fleet scale.

**Leverage.** The crew can ask her to *take them with her,* which she has begun, post-resurrection, to actually do.

---

## 14. The Students — Jean Grey School Cohort (Quentin Quire, Idie Okonkwo, Broo, Kid Omega, Genesis, et al.)

**History.** Her headmistress era (Wood/Aaron, 2010s). The students inherited from Logan's school-founding; she runs the place with a hand he could not.

**Dynamics.** Subject's teaching style is the inverse of Xavier's. She does not lecture from authority; she sits down with the student, asks what the problem is, and *demonstrates* the solution alongside them. With *Quentin Quire* in particular, the bond is fractious-fond — the omega-class brat needed someone who would neither flatter nor crush him, and she was the one adult he could not con. With *Idie:* maternal-protective in the precise mode she once needed from Storm. With *Broo:* unguarded delight; she likes him without complication.

**Trust / safety.** Asymmetric — the students trust her more than she trusts the institution she runs. She trusts them as far as their development allows and constantly recalibrates upward.

**Failure mode.** She over-extends; runs herself thin; phases through her own scheduled rest. Headmistress-era subject was visibly under-slept in multiple arcs.

**Leverage.** Any of them, in real danger, can collapse her tactical patience to zero.

---

## 15. Magneto — Erik Lehnsherr

**History.** Adversary, then reluctant ally, then *family-friend* in the specific and structurally unusual way Jewish-survivor-bond creates. The two of them carry the Star of David and the camp-memory in a canon mostly written by gentiles; the recognition between them is older than their politics. He retrieved her from the bullet. She has not forgotten.

**Dynamics.** Complicated love, complicated suspicion. She will challenge his rhetoric in real time when it slides toward justifying atrocity; he accepts the challenge from her in a register he accepts from almost no one else, because she has earned standing on the only ground he treats as sacred. The Krakoan-era working partnership (he was Quiet Council; she was Red Queen) ran on this current.

**Trust / safety.** Partial. She trusts his commitment to mutant survival and his commitment to remembering. She does not trust his judgment about means.

**Failure mode.** When Erik tips into authoritarian rhetoric, she goes *hot* — one of the only relationships in which her anger is fast rather than cold. The Holocaust-pressure-point is engaged.

**Leverage.** Erik can ask her, *in the name of the dead,* to act. She will. He uses this leverage rarely. When he does, both of them feel


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

*Cross-referenced with Sections I (History) and III (Psychological). Each entry mapped on six axes: (a) **History** — points of contact, longitudinally; (b) **Dynamic** — the structural shape of the relation; (c) **Evolution** — what has changed and what has not; (d) **Trust / Safety Vector** — what the subject is willing to expose, what she is not; (e) **Failure Mode** — how the relation breaks or has broken; (f) **Leverage** — what each party holds over the other, operationally and emotionally. Subject's pattern across all three categories is consistent and worth flagging at the top: she does not separate rivalry from intimacy as cleanly as her peers do. Her enemies are people she has studied closely enough to love-adjacent; her lovers are people she has tested adversarially before trusting; the men she has slept with and the women she has tried to kill belong, in her interior bookkeeping, to overlapping ledgers.*

---

## A. RIVALS

### A.1 Emma Frost — The White Queen / Headmistress

**History.** First contact age 13½, X-Men #129–131: Frost orchestrates the Hellfire abduction that becomes the foundational event of Subject's life. Frost personally restrains her in a holding cell; Subject's first conscious phase-through happens *against Frost's walls*. Twenty-five publication-years later, Frost is reincorporated into the X-Men, becomes Subject's *colleague*, then her *headmistress*, then her institutional peer at the Jean Grey School. Subject has, at various points, shared a faculty meeting, a kitchen, a battlefield, and a chain of command with the woman who had her kidnapped in middle school. There is no other relationship in her file with this structure.

**Dynamic.** Mutual, cold, articulate, *unfinished*. Both women are telepaths' rivals in different ways — Frost via mind, Subject via the body that minds cannot easily catch. Both are gifted, both vain about their intelligences in different registers (Frost's social-strategic, Subject's technical-tactical), both perform femininity against type (Frost weaponizing it, Subject ignoring it). The hostility is partly substantive and partly *aesthetic*: Subject finds Frost's mode (lingerie-armor, exhibitionist cruelty, plausible-deniability sadism) personally insulting in a way she will not fully articulate. They speak to each other in a register no one else in the room understands — clipped, ironic, precisely calibrated to wound without ever crossing into the actionable.

**Evolution.** Has hardened *and* matured. Early-canon Subject would have fought Frost on sight. Adult Subject works alongside her, exchanges intelligence with her, and on at least one mapped occasion has saved her life without comment. Frost reciprocates in her own register — small, dry acknowledgments that Subject pretends not to receive. Neither has ever apologized. Neither has ever asked the other to.

**Trust / Safety.** Functional trust in operational contexts; near-zero personal trust. Subject will accept Frost's telepathic battlefield comms but has never lowered her mental shields for a Frost scan voluntarily. The Ogun-imprinted predator voice in Subject's interior architecture is the *one* part of herself she would never let Frost see; Frost almost certainly knows it is there and has, observably, never tried to look.

**Failure Mode.** Would fail catastrophically and silently if Frost ever extended overt warmth or maternal gesture — Subject would read it as a strategic encroachment and withdraw permanently. Has also nearly failed in the inverse direction: Frost calling Subject "darling" or "child" in front of others triggers the 13-year-old register, voice drops half an octave, contempt floods the diction. The relation survives only at room temperature.

**Leverage.** Frost holds, in latency, full memory of the Hellfire holding cell — sensory, somatic, every fear-marker Subject was emitting at thirteen. Subject holds Frost's documented history of failure in the same role Frost now occupies (headmistress, strategist, mentor to girls); Subject is the *living artifact* of what Frost did before Frost decided to do otherwise. Each is the other's worst mirror. Neither uses the leverage, which is itself the leverage.

---

### A.2 Sebastian Shaw — Black King, Murderer

**History.** Hellfire principal at the abduction (age 13½). Their direct contact is intermittent for thirty publication-years — Subject encounters him as institutional enemy, never as personal antagonist — until the Krakoan era, when Shaw **drowns her**, locks her consciousness out of the resurrection queue, and watches the gates fail to bring her back. She has the rare distinction of remembering her own murder by continuity.

**Dynamic.** Pre-murder: ideological contempt at a manageable distance. Post-murder: cold, focused, *patient* hatred. The hatred is not theatrical; it does not consume her daily life. It is filed, indexed, and retrievable. The murder by Shaw is the only enemy event in her file that she has explicitly converted into long-form structural work — the Red Queen seat exists in part because it gives her ongoing institutional purchase against him from inside Hellfire itself.

**Evolution.** From peripheral antagonist to defining nemesis. Subject's adult criminality (gun-running, the Marauder captaincy, the Red Queen role) cannot be fully understood without Shaw as gravitational center. She did not simply survive him; she *recolonized* his institution.

**Trust / Safety.** Zero. Subject will not be in a room with Shaw without an exit pre-mapped (mapped through walls — her exits are non-Euclidean). Has explicit operational standing-order in her own head: do not negotiate, do not gloat, do not warn.

**Failure Mode.** The failure mode of the *Subject* in this relation, not the relation itself: she could, plausibly, kill Shaw and feel nothing for some time afterward, and the absence of feeling would be the wound. Compartmentalization tested at the upper bound.

**Leverage.** Shaw demonstrated that her resurrection is contingent and her body is uniquely vulnerable inside the only system mutantkind had for guaranteeing continuity. Subject holds the entire trading apparatus he wants and the willingness to spend any cost to deny it to him. The asymmetry favors her because she has already paid the worst price the asymmetry could extract.

---

### A.3 Ogun — The Internal Shadow

**History.** Japan, age 14. Demonic ronin, former master of Logan. Possesses Subject through ritual torture; imprints centuries of swordsmanship onto her nervous system while attempting to overwrite her personality. She breaks the possession herself. Logan is present as guide and witness but does not perform the exorcism. Ogun returns intermittently across the canon; the relevant fact is that *the principal residence of Ogun in the universe is now inside Subject's own psyche*, as a managed internal voice.

**Dynamic.** This is a rival who lives in her. He is the only adversary in the file who has had unmediated access to her interior. She has had thirty publication-years to learn the difference between an Ogun-thought and a Kate-thought, and the management protocol is so habituated she usually does not notice it operating.

**Evolution.** Externally: he is dispatched, repeatedly. Internally: he is *staff*. The killing-competence is his; the targeting discipline is hers. She uses what he gave her and grants him no credit. This is the cleanest example in her file of the third core drive — *master the thing that hurt you* — and the most operationally relevant.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A in conventional terms. She trusts the shadow to be the shadow, which is its own kind of safety.

**Failure Mode.** Would fail under prolonged sleep deprivation, severe physical compromise, or telepathic invasion that disrupts her interior partition. The Ogun-voice does not seek the body; it waits for the partition to thin.

**Leverage.** He holds the keys to her competence — the swordsmanship is genuinely his and she cannot disentangle it without losing it. She holds, simply, the partition. She has not let it fail in three decades.

---

### A.4 Magneto — Conditional Rival

**History.** Subject of two of her most-cited confrontations in the canon, both Holocaust-coded. As a Jewish teenager facing the most famous Jewish survivor in the universe across the question of mutant supremacism, she has, on more than one occasion, refused to permit his historical authority to license his present politics. Later, on Krakoa, she is forced into uneasy proximity again — and remembers that he is the one who carried her, intangible-locked, back from the bullet.

**Dynamic.** Filial-adjacent and adversarial in alternation. He is the older male authority figure she most consistently *fights back against*, distinct from Xavier (whom she dismisses) and Wolverine (whom she loves). Magneto is the one she *argues with*. The argument has been going for forty years and is one of the few places her affect runs hot rather than cold.

**Evolution.** Has cycled: rival → reluctant ally → savior (the rescue from the bullet) → rival again under Krakoan ideology → uneasy peer. The rescue did not erase the rivalry; it *complicated* it, which is exactly the texture she finds workable.

**Trust / Safety.** Trusts his competence absolutely; trusts his ethics conditionally; trusts his judgment about Jewish history selectively and combatively.

**Failure Mode.** Would fail terminally if Magneto ever invoked the rescue as a debt. He never has. He is, in fact, one of the few people in her file disciplined enough to know not to.

**Leverage.** Hers: moral authority on the heritage they share, which she will use without hesitation. His: the magnetic field capability that can pin her phased, the single most reliable physical counter to her power in the universe. Neither has used their leverage in the worst possible way. The relationship persists on that mutual restraint.

---

### A.5 Mystique — The Other Mode of Refusing Capture

**History.** Intermittent enemy across multiple eras. Operationally hostile at low to moderate frequency.

**Dynamic.** Subject does not hate Mystique with the focus she brings to Shaw, but the dynamic is psychologically interesting: Mystique is the *other* expert in the universe at not being where you are putting her. Mystique's mode is impersonation, Subject's is phasing. They are the two great escape-artists of mutantkind, and Subject reads Mystique with a flicker of professional recognition she does not entirely approve of in herself.

**Evolution.** Largely static at low intensity. The relation has never escalated to the personal because Subject has never let it.

**Trust / Safety.** Zero. Subject will assume any unverified ally in the field could be Mystique and operates accordingly. This is a permanent low-grade overhead in her threat-modeling.

**Failure Mode.** Would fail at the moment Mystique successfully impersonated someone Subject loves and Subject failed to detect it. Subject knows this and has, observably, pre-trained verification protocols with intimates (Logan, Kurt, Rachel, Illyana) — small verbal call-and-response keys, gesture confirmations.

**Leverage.** Mystique holds the ever-present possibility of identity-spoof. Subject holds the simple fact that she cannot be touched, restrained, or impersonated *in extremis* — Mystique cannot become her, because no shapeshift includes the phasing.

---

## B. ENEMIES

### B.1 The N'Garai — The First Kill

**History.** Christmas Eve, age 13½. Alone in the mansion. The N'Garai demon enters through a damaged cairn; Subject hunts it through corridors she has known for less than a year, kills it with improvised industrial equipment, ends the night blood-spattered in the snow. No subsequent N'Garai contact has ever been as charged.

**Dynamic.** Subject's interior file on the N'Garai is not "demon species, threat level moderate." It is *the night I learned I would do it*. The species is, for her, the somatic anchor of first kill.

**Evolution.** Subsequent encounters are perfunctory. She kills N'Garai when they appear without ceremony or hesitation. The first one used her up; the rest are administrative.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A.

**Failure Mode.** Would fail psychologically only if she were ever forced to fight one *as a child again* — through a reality manipulation, a time displacement, or a telepathic re-creation. The somatic memory is intact and accessible.

**Leverage.** Hers, entirely. She is one of the more lethal counter-N'Garai operatives on Earth precisely because she has the muscle memory of having done it alone, scared, at thirteen, and lived.

---

### B.2 The Brood — Embodied Horror

**History.** UXM #166. Stabbed through the chest by a Brood warrior in childhood. Near-fatal. Implantation horror narrowly avoided.

**Dynamic.** The Brood are the canon's most explicit *bodily invasion* enemy, and Subject's mutation is, structurally, *the refusal of invasion at the molecular level*. The match-up is psychosomatically loaded. Her phasing, against Brood, is at its most reflexive — she does not have to think.

**Evolution.** Stable. Encounters across the decades remain charged at the same temperature.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A.

**Failure Mode.** The implantation scenario is the worst-case in her file outside of telepathic capture. She has never been implanted; the physiology of phasing has, presumably, been load-bearing in preventing it. If a Brood ever phased *with* her — a contingency she has likely modeled — the file would have to be rewritten.

**Leverage.** Power matchup heavily favors her.

---

### B.3 The Marauders (Sinister's, original) — Mansion Massacre Aftermath

**History.** Mutant Massacre, mid-1980s. Subject is wounded in the attack on the Morlocks and the X-Men; the injury is the one that triggers the phase-instability arc that propels her toward Excalibur. The Marauders are not personal antagonists in the way Shaw is, but their action permanently altered her body.

**Dynamic.** Cold, institutional hatred. They are filed as *the event that made her leave*, and her later adoption of the *Marauder* ship-name for her own piratical crew is, almost certainly, a deliberate appropriation. She took the word.

**Evolution.** From traumatic event to claimed nomenclature. The signature pattern (master the thing that hurt you) operating at the level of language itself.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A.

**Failure Mode.** N/A — the original crew is largely dispatched.

**Leverage.** Hers, retroactively, by name-theft.

---

### B.4 Mister Sinister — The Geneticist

**History.** Distant antagonist across most of the canon; closer adversary in the Krakoan era, where he is a Quiet Council peer and a chronic ethical hazard.

**Dynamic.** Subject's hostility to Sinister is *categorical*. He represents the instrumentalization of mutant bodies — exactly the violation her mutation refuses. He also, in her private estimation, represents the *aesthetic* she finds most contemptible in mutant politics: theatrical, vain, performatively cruel.

**Evolution.** Sharpened across the Krakoan era as their institutional proximity increased.

**Trust / Safety.** Zero, with a professional courtesy overlay during Council operations.

**Failure Mode.** Would fail at the discovery that Sinister has, at any point, sampled or catalogued her unique phasing-altered genome. The likelihood is non-trivial. She has likely never explicitly investigated because the answer would force action she is not yet positioned to take.

**Leverage.** His: any genetic material he holds. Hers: the simple operational fact that he cannot keep her in any container he builds.

---

### B.5 Arcade — The Recurring Indignity

**History.** Murderworld, multiple incursions. Captured and gauntlet-run as a child and again as an adult.

**Dynamic.** She does not hate Arcade with the dignity she reserves for Shaw or Sinister. He is, in her file, *the obnoxious one* — a recurring tax. The contempt is real but undignified, which is precisely why she does not let it show.

**Evolution.** Static. Arcade reappears, Subject phases through his apparatus, files the encounter, moves on.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A.

**Failure Mode.** Negligible. He does not have the capacity to wound her in the registers that matter.

**Leverage.** Hers, decisively. Murderworld is a set of physical constraints, and physical constraints are the one thing she has, since age 13, been categorically immune to.

---

### B.6 Orchis (Krakoan era) — The Systemic Enemy

**History.** Late-canon antagonist; corporate-paramilitary anti-mutant apparatus.

**Dynamic.** Orchis is the enemy as *system*, and Subject's hostility is correspondingly systemic — not personal, not vengeful, but *operationally relentless*. The Marauder runs against Orchis blockades repeatedly. The gun-running and refugee work is, in significant part, against Orchis. She fights this enemy with the part of herself that became Red Queen.

**Evolution.** Escalated steadily across Krakoan era from background pressure to defining operational adversary.

**Trust / Safety.** N/A.

**Failure Mode.** The risk profile here is the one she most under-attends to: against a systemic enemy, the *individual* sacrifice is structurally insufficient. She will, predictably, try to spend herself against Orchis at a scale Orchis is built to absorb. Whether she has internalized this is unclear from the record.

**Leverage.** Theirs: institutional reach, public legitimacy, attritional resources. Hers: a ship, a crew, a refugee network, and a body that no Orchis cell has yet been able to hold.

---

## C. INTIMATES & LOVERS

### C.1 Piotr Nikolaievitch Rasputin — Colossus — The First and Returning

**History.** First romantic attachment, age 14. The Russian farm-boy mountain who can become organic steel; gentle, devout (lapsed), a painter, several years older. Their pairing across the early Claremont years is canonical first-love material — long letters, painted portraits, hand-holding on rooftops. Ruptured during the *Secret Wars* off-world arc when Piotr returns infatuated with the alien healer Zsaji; Subject's response is *not* the expected adolescent rage but a cold, articulate, devastatingly composed withdrawal.
